width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></nobr></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-27">\overline {V^{2}_{n,d}}=4kT \cdot {\rm NEF} \cdot g_{m2} R_{2}^{2}.\eqno{\hbox{(4)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\overline {V^{2}_{n,d}}=4kT \cdot {\rm NEF} \cdot g_{m2} R_{2}^{2}.\eqno{\hbox{(4)}}</span></span></disp-formula></p><p>It is clear that the two noise voltages are uncorrelated. They could not be canceled by a subsequent combining stage. Referring to the output node, the noise due to gain-enhancement stage is <disp-formula id="deqn5" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\overline {V^{2}_{n,out}}=4kT \cdot {\rm NEF} \cdot \left (g_{m2} R_{2}^{2} \,\,{{ g_{m4}^{2}}\over { g_{m5}^{2}}} + g_{m3} R_{3}^{2} \right). \eqno{\hbox{(5)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-28-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-28">\overline {V^{2}_{n,out}}=4kT \cdot {\rm NEF} \cdot \left (g_{m2} R_{2}^{2} \,\,{{ g_{m4}^{2}}\over { g_{m5}^{2}}} + g_{m3} R_{3}^{2} \right). \eqno{\hbox{(5)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\overline {V^{2}_{n,out}}=4kT \cdot {\rm NEF} \cdot \left (g_{m2} R_{2}^{2} \,\,{{ g_{m4}^{2}}\over { g_{m5}^{2}}} + g_{m3} R_{3}^{2} \right). \eqno{\hbox{(5)}}</span></span></disp-formula>Equation <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn5" href="#deqn5" class="fulltext-link">(5)</a> shows the noise contribution of <i>M</i>2–<i>R</i>2 is of higher weight than that of <i>M</i>3–<i>R</i>3.</p><p>The output noise of matching device <i>M</i>1 can be canceled by properly design of the gain in the two feed-forward paths. In <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 2</a>, the cancellation condition is given by <disp-formula id="deqn6" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{s} \cdot g_{m2}R_{2} \cdot {{ g_{m4}}\over { g_{m5}}} = (R_{f} + R_{s}) \cdot g_{m3}R_{3}.\eqno{\hbox{(6)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-29-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-29">R_{s} \cdot g_{m2}R_{2} \cdot {{ g_{m4}}\over { g_{m5}}} = (R_{f} + R_{s}) \cdot g_{m3}R_{3}.\eqno{\hbox{(6)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">R_{s} \cdot g_{m2}R_{2} \cdot {{ g_{m4}}\over { g_{m5}}} = (R_{f} + R_{s}) \cdot g_{m3}R_{3}.\eqno{\hbox{(6)}}</span></span></disp-formula> Therefore, <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn5" href="#deqn5" class="fulltext-link">(5)</a> becomes <disp-formula id="deqn7" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\overline {V^{2}_{n,out}}=\alpha \cdot \left (g_{m3} R_{3} \right)^{2} \,\, \left [ {{ \left (1+ {\displaystyle{{ R_{f}}\over { R_{s}}}}\right)^{2}}\over { g_{m2}}} + {{ 1}\over { g_{m3}}} \right ] \eqno{\hbox{(7)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-30-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-30">\overline {V^{2}_{n,out}}=\alpha \cdot \left (g_{m3} R_{3} \right)^{2} \,\, \left [ {{ \left (1+ {\displaystyle{{ R_{f}}\over { R_{s}}}}\right)^{2}}\over { g_{m2}}} + {{ 1}\over { g_{m3}}} \right ] \eqno{\hbox{(7)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\overline {V^{2}_{n,out}}=\alpha \cdot \left (g_{m3} R_{3} \right)^{2} \,\, \left [ {{ \left (1+ {\displaystyle{{ R_{f}}\over { R_{s}}}}\right)^{2}}\over { g_{m2}}} + {{ 1}\over { g_{m3}}} \right ] \eqno{\hbox{(7)}}</span></span></disp-formula> where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\alpha = 4kT \cdot {\rm NEF}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-31-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-31">\alpha = 4kT \cdot {\rm NEF}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. From <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn7" href="#deqn7" class="fulltext-link">(7)</a>, the noise contribution of the gain-enhancement stage can be minimized when <disp-formula id="deqn8" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{{ g_{m2}}\over { g_{m3}}}=\left (1+ {{ R_{f}}\over { R_{s}}}\right)^{2} \eqno{\hbox{(8)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-32-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-32">{{ g_{m2}}\over { g_{m3}}}=\left (1+ {{ R_{f}}\over { R_{s}}}\right)^{2} \eqno{\hbox{(8)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">{{ g_{m2}}\over { g_{m3}}}=\left (1+ {{ R_{f}}\over { R_{s}}}\right)^{2} \eqno{\hbox{(8)}}</span></span></disp-formula> and the minimum output noise voltage is <disp-formula id="deqn9" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\overline {V^{2}_{n,out}}=2 \cdot \alpha \cdot g_{m3}R_{3}^{2}.\eqno{\hbox{(9)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-33-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-33">\overline {V^{2}_{n,out}}=2 \cdot \alpha \cdot g_{m3}R_{3}^{2}.\eqno{\hbox{(9)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\overline {V^{2}_{n,out}}=2 \cdot \alpha \cdot g_{m3}R_{3}^{2}.\eqno{\hbox{(9)}}</span></span></disp-formula></p><p>It is interesting to note that these results are independent of the following noise combining stage. Note that <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn7" href="#deqn7" class="fulltext-link">(7)</a>–<a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn8" href="#deqn8" class="fulltext-link"></a><a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn9" href="#deqn9" class="fulltext-link">(9)</a> are obtained under the noise cancellation condition for the input matching device. From <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn8" href="#deqn8" class="fulltext-link">(8)</a>, the condition <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn6" href="#deqn6" class="fulltext-link">(6)</a> can be simplified as <disp-formula id="deqn10" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{{ g_{m5}}\over { g_{m4}}} = {{ R_{2}}\over { R_{3}}}\left (1+ {{ R_{f}}\over { R_{s}}}\right).\eqno{\hbox{(10)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-34-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-34">{{ g_{m5}}\over { g_{m4}}} = {{ R_{2}}\over { R_{3}}}\left (1+ {{ R_{f}}\over { R_{s}}}\right).\eqno{\hbox{(10)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">{{ g_{m5}}\over { g_{m4}}} = {{ R_{2}}\over { R_{3}}}\left (1+ {{ R_{f}}\over { R_{s}}}\right).\eqno{\hbox{(10)}}</span></span></disp-formula></p><p>Considering the overall small-signal gain, we have <disp-formula id="deqn11" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\eqalignno{A_{V} =&amp;\, -A_{v,1} \cdot g_{m3}R_{3} + g_{m2}R_{2} \cdot {{ g_{m4}}\over { g_{m5}}} \cr =&amp;\, \left (-A_{v,1}+1+ {{ R_{f}}\over { R_{s}}}\right) \cdot g_{m3}R_{3}. &amp;{\hbox{(11)}}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-35-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-35">\eqalignno{A_{V} =&amp;\, -A_{v,1} \cdot g_{m3}R_{3} + g_{m2}R_{2} \cdot {{ g_{m4}}\over { g_{m5}}} \cr =&amp;\, \left (-A_{v,1}+1+ {{ R_{f}}\over { R_{s}}}\right) \cdot g_{m3}R_{3}. &amp;{\hbox{(11)}}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\eqalignno{A_{V} =&amp;\, -A_{v,1} \cdot g_{m3}R_{3} + g_{m2}R_{2} \cdot {{ g_{m4}}\over { g_{m5}}} \cr =&amp;\, \left (-A_{v,1}+1+ {{ R_{f}}\over { R_{s}}}\right) \cdot g_{m3}R_{3}. &amp;{\hbox{(11)}}}</span></span></disp-formula> where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">A_{v,1}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-36-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-36">A_{v,1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the gain of the input matching stage <disp-formula id="deqn12" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">A_{v,1}=- {{ (g_{m1}R_{f}-1)R_{1}}\over { R_{f}+R_{1}}}.\eqno{\hbox{(12)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-37-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-37">A_{v,1}=- {{ (g_{m1}R_{f}-1)R_{1}}\over { R_{f}+R_{1}}}.\eqno{\hbox{(12)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">A_{v,1}=- {{ (g_{m1}R_{f}-1)R_{1}}\over { R_{f}+R_{1}}}.\eqno{\hbox{(12)}}</span></span></disp-formula> The result is intuitive, a gain-enhancement factor <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">g_{m3}R_{3}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-38-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-38">g_{m3}R_{3}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is achieved comparing with the original architecture [see <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1(a)</a>] whose gain is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-A_{v,1}+1+{R_{f}}/{R_{s}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-39-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-39">-A_{v,1}+1+{R_{f}}/{R_{s}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>.</p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec3b"><h3>B. Properties and Limitations</h3><div class="section_2" id="sec3b1"><h4>1. Design Flexibility</h4><p>The additional parameters in the noise cancellation equations permit more design flexibilities. Generally, the original noise cancellation technique is a two-step operation: noise sensing and combining/canceling. Besides the system requirements, the design parameters of the two stages are further limited by each other (to fulfill the noise cancellation condition). The proposed technique provides a three-step operation: noise sensing, amplification, and combining. The noise cancellation requirement can be fulfilled with more design flexibilities. From this point of view, the amplification stage acts as a “buffer,” which reduces the coupling between the noise sensing and combining stages.</p><p>A good example is the design of the output combining stage, which dominates the noise performance of the original architecture. In <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1(a)</a>, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">g_{m3}/g_{m4}=A_{v,2}=(1+R_{f}/R_{s})</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-40-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-40">g_{m3}/g_{m4}=A_{v,2}=(1+R_{f}/R_{s})</script></tex-math></inline-formula> should be fulfilled. This poses a rather restrained headroom in minimizing the output noise due to combining stage. However, from <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn6" href="#deqn6" class="fulltext-link">(6)</a> or <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn10" href="#deqn10" class="fulltext-link">(10)</a>, the additional parameters in the proposed architecture (<a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 2</a>), e.g., <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-41-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-41">R_{2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{3}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-42-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-42">R_{3}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, provide a more flexible requirement of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">g_{m4}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-43-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-43">g_{m4}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">g_{m5}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-44-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-44">g_{m5}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. This can be used to minimize the noise of combining stage. As a result, the additional noise contributed by the gain-enhancement stage can be partially compensated by the improved combining stage.</p><p>The design flexibilities offered in the proposed circuit can also be understood from the point of view of tradeoff. The fundamental tradeoff between the NF and source impedance matching exists in a wideband LNA. To relax this tradeoff, the original noise cancellation technique introduces one more parameter of gain. The simultaneous noise and matching performance is achieved by the tradeoff with gain through the noise cancellation equations. By inserting the amplification stage as proposed here, the tradeoff among noise, matching, and gain is relaxed, while the cost is the increased noise and distortion contributed by the additional amplification stage.</p><p>From <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn9" href="#deqn9" class="fulltext-link">(9)</a> and <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn11" href="#deqn11" class="fulltext-link">(11)</a>, the amplification stage determines the tradeoff between the gain enhancement and the additional noise contribution. Although the gain can be improved by increasing <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">g_{m3}R_{3}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-45-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-45">g_{m3}R_{3}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, the NF of the overall LNA also increases, and vice versa. To maintain a reasonable NF, the enhanced gain cannot be arbitrarily high. The optimal design of circuit parameters depends on the target specification.</p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec3b2"><h4>2. Mismatch</h4><p>An important issue introduced by the gain-enhancement stage is mismatch. In principle, the noise canceling architecture employs two feed-forward paths, device mismatch between them will degrade the noise canceling. When the amplification stage is not used, it is shown that the two-step noise canceling is robust to device parameter variations <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref27" id="context_ref_27_3b2">[27]</a>. With the amplification stage in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 2</a>, the excess noise factor contributed by the matching device can be expressed as <disp-formula id="deqn13" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\rm EF}_{\rm MD}= {\rm NEF}\,\, {{ \left [(R_{f}+R_{s})\,A_{v,3}-R_{s}\,A_{v2}\,A_{v4}\right ]^{2}}\over { R_{s} A_{V}^{2}}} \eqno{\hbox{(13)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-46-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-46">{\rm EF}_{\rm MD}= {\rm NEF}\,\, {{ \left [(R_{f}+R_{s})\,A_{v,3}-R_{s}\,A_{v2}\,A_{v4}\right ]^{2}}\over { R_{s} A_{V}^{2}}} \eqno{\hbox{(13)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">{\rm EF}_{\rm MD}= {\rm NEF}\,\, {{ \left [(R_{f}+R_{s})\,A_{v,3}-R_{s}\,A_{v2}\,A_{v4}\right ]^{2}}\over { R_{s} A_{V}^{2}}} \eqno{\hbox{(13)}}</span></span></disp-formula> where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">A_{v,2}=g_{m2}R_{2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-47-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-47">A_{v,2}=g_{m2}R_{2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">A_{v,3}=g_{m3}R_{3}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-48-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-48">A_{v,3}=g_{m3}R_{3}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> denote the gain of the two paths, and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">A_{v,4}=g_{m4}/g_{m5}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-49-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-49">A_{v,4}=g_{m4}/g_{m5}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> denotes the gain of noise combining stage. <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">A_{V}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-50-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-50">A_{V}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the overall gain as discussed in <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn11" href="#deqn11" class="fulltext-link">(11)</a>.</p><p>Note that <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn13" href="#deqn13" class="fulltext-link">(13)</a> is obtained by assuming equal <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\rm NEF</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-51-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-51">\rm NEF</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">Z_{\rm IN}=R_{s}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-52-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-52">Z_{\rm IN}=R_{s}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. Since the nominal values of the parameters in <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn13" href="#deqn13" class="fulltext-link">(13)</a> are determined by <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn5" href="#deqn5" class="fulltext-link">(5)</a>–<a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn6" href="#deqn6" class="fulltext-link"></a><a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn7" href="#deqn7" class="fulltext-link"></a><a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn8" href="#deqn8" class="fulltext-link"></a><a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn9" href="#deqn9" class="fulltext-link"></a><a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn10" href="#deqn10" class="fulltext-link"></a><a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn11" href="#deqn11" class="fulltext-link"></a><a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn12" href="#deqn12" class="fulltext-link">(12)</a>, mathematical expressions of the mismatch effect may not be intuitional. Here, the numerical simulation is performed assuming <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\rm NEF}=1.5</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-53-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-53">{\rm NEF}=1.5</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{f}/R_{s}=3</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-54-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-54">R_{f}/R_{s}=3</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 3</a> shows the noise contribution of the matching device (<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\rm EF}_{\rm MD}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-55-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-55">{\rm EF}_{\rm MD}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>) resulting from the variations of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">A_{v,2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-56-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-56">A_{v,2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">A_{v,3}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-57-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-57">A_{v,3}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. It is shown that <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\rm EF}_{\rm MD}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-58-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-58">{\rm EF}_{\rm MD}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is kept at 0 as long as the variations of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">A_{v,2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-59-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-59">A_{v,2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">A_{v,3}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-60-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-60">A_{v,3}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> are equal to each other; i.e., it is not the absolute variation, but the relative variation that degrades the noise canceling. In other words, the mismatch between the two amplification paths degrades noise canceling. With regard to the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\rm EF}_{\rm MD}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-61-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-61">{\rm EF}_{\rm MD}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> margin of 0.1, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\pm {\hbox{6}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-62-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-62">\pm {\hbox{6}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>% mismatch between <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">A_{v,2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-63-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-63">A_{v,2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">A_{v,3}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-64-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-64">A_{v,3}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is allowed.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig3"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-fig-3-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-fig-3-small.gif" alt="Figure 3" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 3. </b><fig><p>Numerical simulation of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\rm EF}_{\rm MD}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-65-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-65">{\rm EF}_{\rm MD}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> with regard to the variations of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">A_{v,2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-66-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-66">A_{v,2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">A_{v,3}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-67-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-67">A_{v,3}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4339618/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>The result obtained in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref27" id="context_ref_27_3b2">[27]</a> shows that <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\pm {\hbox{20}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-68-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-68">\pm {\hbox{20}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>% variations in the noise sensing and combining stages are allowed for an <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\rm EF}_{\rm MD}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-69-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-69">{\rm EF}_{\rm MD}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> margin of 0.1. However, the additional gain stage is relatively sensitive to device parameter mismatch. Therefore, the mismatch robustness of the overall LNA is limited by this stage, which is another price that is paid for the increased gain and design flexibilities.</p></div></div><div class="section_2" id="sec3c"><h3>C. Circuit Design</h3><p>Based on the design equations <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn5" href="#deqn5" class="fulltext-link">(5)</a>–<a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn6" href="#deqn6" class="fulltext-link"></a><a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn7" href="#deqn7" class="fulltext-link"></a><a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn8" href="#deqn8" class="fulltext-link"></a><a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn9" href="#deqn9" class="fulltext-link"></a><a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn10" href="#deqn10" class="fulltext-link"></a><a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn11" href="#deqn11" class="fulltext-link"></a><a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn12" href="#deqn12" class="fulltext-link">(12)</a>, the circuit parameters of <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 2</a> can be optimized. A standard 1.2-V 0.13-<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\mu{\hbox {m}} </span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-70-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-70">\mu{\hbox {m}} </script></tex-math></inline-formula> CMOS process is used in this design. The target specifications are: 1) 20-dB voltage gain; 2) <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">&lt; {\hbox {3-dB}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-71-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-71">&lt; {\hbox {3-dB}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> NF; and 3) input impedance matching to 50 <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Omega </span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-72-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-72">\Omega </script></tex-math></inline-formula> over 3.1–10.6 GHz. The <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-{\hbox {3-dB}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-73-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-73">-{\hbox {3-dB}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> bandwidth of voltage gain is also of high importance. For this architecture, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-{\hbox {3-dB}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-74-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-74">-{\hbox {3-dB}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> bandwidth is mainly decided by parasitic capacitances. Here, minimizing parasitics will be taken into considerations, but no bandwidth target is set, as the current design phase is dedicated to gain and noise performances.</p><p><inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{f}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-75-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-75">R_{f}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> cannot be very large, although large <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{f}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-76-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-76">R_{f}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> offers high gain. From <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn8" href="#deqn8" class="fulltext-link">(8)</a>, a large <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{f}/R_{s}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-77-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-77">R_{f}/R_{s}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> results in a huge ratio of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">g_{m2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-78-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-78">g_{m2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">g_{m3}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-79-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-79">g_{m3}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. Considering that <i>M</i>2 and <i>M</i>3 share the same current branch, it is quite difficult to obtain a huge <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">g_{m2}/g_{m3}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-80-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-80">g_{m2}/g_{m3}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. Secondly, the input matching requires a small <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{f}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-81-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-81">R_{f}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> as compared to the input parasitics <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{\rm in}=C_{{\rm gs}1}+C_{{\rm gs}2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-82-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-82">C_{\rm in}=C_{{\rm gs}1}+C_{{\rm gs}2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> at several gigahertz frequencies. As a result, the gain of input stage <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">A_{v,1}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-83-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-83">A_{v,1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is a small value. Furthermore, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{\rm in}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-84-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-84">C_{\rm in}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> should be kept small for precise noise sensing, otherwise noise cancellation degrades. Thus, large <i>M</i>1 and <i>M</i>2 should be avoided. High <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">g_{m1}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-85-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-85">g_{m1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">g_{m2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-86-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-86">g_{m2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> have to be obtained by increasing the respective drain current. Here, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{f}/R_{s}=3</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-87-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-87">R_{f}/R_{s}=3</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">A_{v,1}\approx 0.97</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-88-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-88">A_{v,1}\approx 0.97</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. The input stage draws 3 mA with 1.2-V supply voltage.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig4"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-fig-4-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-fig-4-small.gif" alt="Figure 4" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 4. </b><fig><p>Simulated performance of the gain-enhanced noise canceling circuit in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 2</a>.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4339618/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>Equation <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn8" href="#deqn8" class="fulltext-link">(8)</a> then becomes <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">g_{m2}/g_{m3}=16</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-89-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-89">g_{m2}/g_{m3}=16</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. It is still a large ratio in a practical circuit. On the other hand, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">g_{m2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-90-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-90">g_{m2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">g_{m3}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-91-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-91">g_{m3}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> cannot be selected according only to this condition. A large <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">g_{m2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-92-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-92">g_{m2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> requires high current and large size of <i>M</i>2, which is not favorable for the sake of power and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{\rm in}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-93-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-93">C_{\rm in}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, respectively, while a low <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">g_{m3}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-94-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-94">g_{m3}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> degrades the overall gain. In fact, <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn8" href="#deqn8" class="fulltext-link">(8)</a> considers only the minimum noise contribution of the gain-enhancement stage. Thus, there are tradeoffs in the design of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">g_{m2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-95-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-95">g_{m2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">g_{m3}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-96-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-96">g_{m3}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. Simulation shows a reasonable ratio <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">g_{m2}/g_{m3}=50~ {\hbox{mS}}/10 ~{\hbox{mS}}=5</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-97-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-97">g_{m2}/g_{m3}=50~ {\hbox{mS}}/10 ~{\hbox{mS}}=5</script></tex-math></inline-formula> provides most balanced performances. To maintain a minimum <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{\rm in}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-98-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-98">C_{\rm in}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, large <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">g_{m2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-99-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-99">g_{m2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> cannot be realized only by increasing the size of <i>M</i>2. Thus, <i>R</i>8 is added in parallel with <i>M</i>3–<i>R</i>3 to provide more current to <i>M</i>2. Meanwhile, as <i>M</i>3 draws only part of the drain current of <i>M</i>2, a larger <i>R</i>3 within the same voltage headroom is available, leading to a larger available gain-enhancement factor. Note that <i>R</i>8's noise contribution is slight due to the large <i>C</i>2.</p><p>The parameters of the combining stage depends on <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn6" href="#deqn6" class="fulltext-link">(6)</a>. In an ideal case, the output impedance matching could be achieved <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref27" id="context_ref_27_3c">[27]</a>. However, the stringent noise budget permits little freedom in doing so. In this design, the output is not intentionally matched, which would certainly affect the overall power gain.</p><p><a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig4" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 4</a> shows the simulated performance of a gain-enhanced noise canceling LNA. Note that 2-pF capacitors are used for input and output coupling. For the bandwidth of 3.1–10.6 GHz, 2.4–2.8-dB NF, 12–15.6-dB gain, and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">S_{11} &lt; -10~{\hbox {dB}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-100-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-100">S_{11} &lt; -10~{\hbox {dB}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> input matching are achieved. The whole circuit draws 10.8 mA from a 1.2-V supply.</p></div></div>
<div class="section" id="sec4"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION IV.</div><h2>Capacitive Peaking</h2></div><p>An on-chip inductor is widely used to improve the bandwidth performance of the broadband LNA. For the proposed circuit in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 2</a>, inductors can also be used for bandwidth extension. A possible example is shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig5" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 5</a>, where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">L1</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-101-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-101">L1</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">L2</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-102-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-102">L2</script></tex-math></inline-formula> are employed in the input stage. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig6" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 6</a> shows the simulation result with <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">L_{1}=0.6~{\hbox {nH}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-103-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-103">L_{1}=0.6~{\hbox {nH}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">L_{2}=1.5~{\hbox {nH}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-104-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-104">L_{2}=1.5~{\hbox {nH}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. Compared with <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig4" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 4</a>, the bandwidth is increased and very good gain flatness is obtained. Furthermore, the input matching and noise performance are improved significantly. This is because the input capacitance is the dominate limitation for both impedance matching and noise sensing, the inductors in the input stage decrease such an effect at high frequencies.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig5"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-fig-5-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-fig-5-small.gif" alt="Figure 5" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 5. </b><fig><p>Example of a gain-enhanced noise canceling LNA with inductor peaking.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4339618/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig6"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-fig-6-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-fig-6-small.gif" alt="Figure 6" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 6. </b><fig><p>Simulation result of <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig5" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 5</a> with <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">L_{1}=0.6~{\hbox {nH}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-105-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-105">L_{1}=0.6~{\hbox {nH}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">L_{2}=1.5~{\hbox {nH}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-106-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-106">L_{2}=1.5~{\hbox {nH}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4339618/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>In <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref26" id="context_ref_26_4">[26]</a>, the design employs an extra stage after the noise canceling LNA to improve the gain, where five inductors are used and the simulated NF is well above 3.5 dB. The comparison shows the proposed gain-enhancement technique permits better noise performance with less inductors. Meanwhile, the inductors used here can possibly be implemented with bond-wire inductance <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref31" id="context_ref_31_4">[31]</a>.</p><p>However, the on-chip inductor occupies a large silicon area. For applications where the area requirement is stringent, e.g., multiple-input multiple-output (MIMO) transceiver, the inductor-based design is not favorable, while the use of bond-wire inductance depends on the circuit configuration and is severely limited by the packaging and modeling technologies. Hence, in this paper, the capacitive peaking technique is employed for bandwidth extension, which has been successfully implemented in transimpedance amplifiers <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref32" id="context_ref_32_4">[32]</a>.</p><p><a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig7" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 7</a> shows the schematic of a capacitive peaking common-source stage. It is adopted from the source degeneration architecture. The gain of this stage can be written as <disp-formula id="deqn14" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">A_{v}=- {{ R_{L}}\over { {\displaystyle{{ 1}\over { g_{m}}}}+R_{p} \Vert C_{p}}}=- {{ R_{L}}\over { {\displaystyle{{ 1}\over { g_{m}}}}+ {\displaystyle{{ 1}\over { {\displaystyle{{ 1}\over { R_{p}}}}+j\omega C_{p}}}}}}.\eqno{\hbox{(14)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-107-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-107">A_{v}=- {{ R_{L}}\over { {\displaystyle{{ 1}\over { g_{m}}}}+R_{p} \Vert C_{p}}}=- {{ R_{L}}\over { {\displaystyle{{ 1}\over { g_{m}}}}+ {\displaystyle{{ 1}\over { {\displaystyle{{ 1}\over { R_{p}}}}+j\omega C_{p}}}}}}.\eqno{\hbox{(14)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">A_{v}=- {{ R_{L}}\over { {\displaystyle{{ 1}\over { g_{m}}}}+R_{p} \Vert C_{p}}}=- {{ R_{L}}\over { {\displaystyle{{ 1}\over { g_{m}}}}+ {\displaystyle{{ 1}\over { {\displaystyle{{ 1}\over { R_{p}}}}+j\omega C_{p}}}}}}.\eqno{\hbox{(14)}}</span></span></disp-formula> For the first order, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{p}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-108-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-108">C_{p}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> provides an increasing <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">A_{v}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-109-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-109">A_{v}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> regarding frequency. This effect can be understood conceptually from the resistive degeneration architecture. It is well known that a source-degenerative resistor degrades gain and improves linearity, while <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{p}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-110-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-110">C_{p}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> provides a signal path in parallel with <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{p}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-111-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-111">R_{p}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> to the ground. At high frequencies, the path through <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{p}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-112-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-112">C_{p}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> has a low impedance. Thus, the resistive degeneration effect is degraded by <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{p}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-113-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-113">C_{p}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> with the increase of frequency, resulting in an increasing gain characteristic. However, the gain cannot exceed the value of a common-source stage without source degeneration, which actually sets the upper bound of the gain of capacitive peaking stage. In other words, capacitive peaking is rather an approach to controlling the gain flatness at the cost of absolute gain value. Note that a negative impedance is created, therefore, the stability of the capacitive peaking LNA should be carefully examined.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig7"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-fig-7-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-fig-7-small.gif" alt="Figure 7" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 7. </b><fig><p>Schematic of a capacitive peaking gain stage.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4339618/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>Besides gain degradation, the degeneration resistor and capacitor also affect the impedance matching and noise-canceling conditions. Thus, capacitive peaking should be carefully verified in the circuit. For the gain-enhanced noise canceling architecture in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 2</a>, the input transistor <i>M</i>1 is of the highest impact on the noise and matching performance, thus, source degeneration is not used in <i>M</i>1. The gain of the <i>M</i>3–<i>R</i>3 stage determines the gain-enhancement factor directly, it is then efficient to apply capacitive peaking technique in <i>M</i>3–<i>R</i>3. <i>M</i>2–<i>R</i>2 and <i>M</i>4–<i>M</i>5 also adopt the capacitive degeneration.</p><p>The use of source degeneration depletes the available voltage headroom, especially for the gain-enhancement stage, which actually contains two stacked common-source amplifiers with source degeneration. Although the presence of <i>R</i>8 redistributes the current flow in the branch, a 1.2-V supply cannot safely provide sufficient voltage headroom for <i>M</i>2 and <i>M</i>3. Hence, 1.5-V supply voltage is used. Meanwhile, a tail resistor <i>R</i>5 is added to control the dc current of <i>M</i>4 and <i>M</i>5.</p><p><a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig8" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 8</a> shows the final circuit of the proposed LNA. The simulation result is shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig9" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 9</a>. With 1.5-V supply voltage, the circuit parameters are redesigned to preserve a similar condition as presented in <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec3c" class="fulltext-link">Section III-C</a>. At the price of degraded gain of 12 dB and minimum NF of 2.8 dB, simulation shows the capacitive peaking technique enhances gain flatness within 3 dB over 2.6–12 GHz. The stability is carefully verified, which shows the LNA is unconditionally stable.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig8"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-fig-8-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-fig-8-small.gif" alt="Figure 8" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 8. </b><fig><p>Complete schematic of gain-enhanced noise canceling LNA with capacitive peaking.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4339618/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig9"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-fig-9-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-fig-9-small.gif" alt="Figure 9" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 9. </b><fig><p>Simulation result of the proposed noise canceling LNA with gain-enhancement and capacitive peaking.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4339618/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec5"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION V.</div><h2>Measurement Results and Discussions</h2></div><p>The LNA circuit was fabricated in a two-poly eight-metal 0.13-<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\mu{\hbox {m}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-114-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-114">\mu{\hbox {m}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> triple-well CMOS technology. The cutoff frequency <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">f_{T}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-115-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-115">f_{T}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> of the nMOS transistor is over 90 GHz. The triple-well process employs a buried deep <i>N</i>-well in the <i>P</i>-substrate to form an isolated <i>P</i>-well, which reduces the noise coupling through substrate. For better linearity, each <i>P</i>-well and deep <i>N</i>-well of an nMOS transistor is biased to ground or supply voltage through a 5-<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\hbox{k}}\Omega </span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-116-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-116">{\hbox{k}}\Omega </script></tex-math></inline-formula> resistor <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref33" id="context_ref_33_5">[33]</a>. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig10" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 10</a> shows a die microphotograph of the fabricated LNA. As the passive devices used are only resistors and capacitors, the LNA core occupies only 221 <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\mu{\hbox {m}}\times\,</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-117-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-117">\mu{\hbox {m}}\times\,</script></tex-math></inline-formula>228 <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\mu{\hbox {m}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-118-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-118">\mu{\hbox {m}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, which is among the smallest designs. With test pads, the LNA chip occupies 415 <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\mu{\hbox {m}}\times\,</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-119-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-119">\mu{\hbox {m}}\times\,</script></tex-math></inline-formula>415<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\mu{\hbox {m}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-120-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-120">\mu{\hbox {m}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> area.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig10"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-fig-10-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-fig-10-small.gif" alt="Figure 10" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 10. </b><fig><p>Die microphotograph of the fabricated LNA chip.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4339618/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig11"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-fig-11-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-fig-11-small.gif" alt="Figure 11" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 11. </b><fig><p>Measured performance of the fabricated LNA.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4339618/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>The measurement of the LNA chip is performed on-wafer using Cascade G–S–G RF probes. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig11" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 11</a> shows the measured performance of the fabricated LNA. The maximum gain is 11 dB and the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-{\hbox {3-dB}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-121-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-121">-{\hbox {3-dB}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> bandwidth is 2–9.6 GHz. The frequency-dependent difference compared to simulated power gain is most likely due to the extra parasitic capacitances in the real chip. At the same time, the gain degradation may also result from the device parameter variations. From <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig8" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 8</a>, the entire circuit is self-biased, and the current in each branch is directly determined by the supply voltage and device parameters. The latter is process dependent and cannot be controlled manually. Measurement shows the LNA draws a total of 12.65 mA instead of the simulated 12 mA, which is clear evidence of the existence of inaccurate resistors. Using mirrored current sources can improve the current accuracy; however, the transistors are not comparable with resistors in terms of wideband performance.</p><p><inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">S_{11}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-122-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-122">S_{11}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is less than <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-{\hbox {8.3 dB}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-123-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-123">-{\hbox {8.3 dB}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> over 2–9.6 GHz, which shows the resistive shunt feedback can effectively offer a 50-<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Omega </span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-124-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-124">\Omega </script></tex-math></inline-formula> matching resistance over the UWB frequencies. The reverse transmission gain <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">S_{12}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-125-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-125">S_{12}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is below <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-{\hbox {50 dB}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-126-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-126">-{\hbox {50 dB}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> (not shown here).</p><p>The NF is within 3.6–4.8 dB over 2–9.6 GHz, which is approximately 1 dB higher than the simulated result. As previously discussed, this is probably due to the mismatch between the two gain-enhancement paths. The inaccuracy of the noise model can also create discrepancy. The average NF over 2–9.6 GHz is 4 dB.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig12"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-fig-12-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-fig-12-small.gif" alt="Figure 12" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 12. </b><fig><p>Linearity measurement in terms of ICP, IIP2, and IIP3 at 6 GHz.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4339618/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>The linearity of the UWB LNA is very important since the UWB receiver suffers from a wide range of interferences <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref18" id="context_ref_18_5">[18]</a>. As shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig12" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 12</a>, at 6 GHz, the input 1-dB compression point (ICP) is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-{\hbox{16.5 dBm}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-127-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-127">-{\hbox{16.5 dBm}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. The input third-order intercept point (IIP3) is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-{\hbox{7.2 dBm}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-128-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-128">-{\hbox{7.2 dBm}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, where a two-tone test is performed at 6 GHz with 100-MHz tone spacing. A further two-tone test is performed with <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">f_{1}=6~{\hbox{GHz}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-129-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-129">f_{1}=6~{\hbox{GHz}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">f_{2}=2.4~{\hbox{GHz}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-130-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-130">f_{2}=2.4~{\hbox{GHz}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> since their second-order intermodulation product falls in the signal band. The input second-order intercept point (IIP2) in this case is 21 dBm. The linearity of this LNA is comparable to the reported inductor based designs.</p><p>Note that since the output is not intentionally matched to 50 <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\ \Omega </span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-131-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-131">\ \Omega </script></tex-math></inline-formula>, and there is no additional buffer stage employed, the power gain and NF performances achieved here have been degraded by the 50-<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Omega </span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-132-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-132">\Omega </script></tex-math></inline-formula> termination of test equipment. The maximum voltage gain calculated in this design is around 14 dB, which is relatively low since the capacitive peaking degrades gain severely. When the area requirement is not stringent, the inductor-based approach, as shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig6" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 6</a>, is much more preferred, and the bandwidth and noise performance should be improved as compared to this design.</p><p><a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">Table I</a> shows a summary of measured performances of the inductorless LNA. The comparison with other reported state-of-the-art amplifier designs is also presented. It is shown that the LNA designed without an inductor achieves comparable performance with other reported inductor-based LNA designs over the ultra-wide bandwidth. The presented LNA is suitable for UWB wireless receiver front-ends.
</p><div class="figure figure-full table" id="table1"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Table I </b>Summary of the LNA Performances and Comparison With Reported State-of-the-Art Designs<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-table-1-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4339599/4339618/4339618-table-1-small.gif" alt="Table I" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div>
<p></p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec6"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION VI.</div><h2>Conclusion</h2></div><p>This paper has demonstrated an inductorless LNA design capable of UWB applications. Without on-chip inductors, the ultra-wide <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-{\hbox {3-dB}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-133-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-133">-{\hbox {3-dB}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> bandwidth was achieved by a syncretic adoption of thermal noise canceling, capacitor peaking, and current reuse. A gain-enhanced architecture based on the noise canceling principle was proposed and detailed analysis of this circuit was performed. Modified noise canceling equations were presented. Capacitive peaking is not able to enhance the absolute bandwidth; however, it is an effective way of controlling the gain flatness. Fabricated in a 0.13-<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\mu{\hbox {m}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-134-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-134">\mu{\hbox {m}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> triple-well CMOS process, the LNA achieves a small-signal gain of 11 dB and a <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-{\hbox {3-dB}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-135-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-135">-{\hbox {3-dB}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> bandwidth of 2–9.6 GHz with <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">S_{11} &lt; -8.3~{\hbox {dB}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-136-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-136">S_{11} &lt; -8.3~{\hbox {dB}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> in-band impedance matching. The NF is 3.6–4.8 dB over 2–9.6 GHz. The LNA consumes 19 mW from a low supply voltage of 1.5 V. The performance is comparable and sometimes better than the reported inductor-based designs. Benefiting from its inductorless architecture, the LNA core occupies only a 0.05-<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\hbox {mm}}^{2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-137-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-137">{\hbox {mm}}^{2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> die area, which is among the smallest UWB LNA reported in the literature.</p></div>
<h3>ACKNOWLEDGMENT</h3><p>The authors would like to thank Dr. Y. Hu and W. M. Lim, both with the Nanyang Technological University, Singapore, for their help with the on-wafer measurements.</p></div></div>

			</li><!-- end ngRepeat: keyword in vm.contextData.keywords --><li class="doc-all-keywords-list-item ng-scope" ng-repeat="keyword in vm.contextData.keywords">
				<strong class="ng-binding">INSPEC: Non-Controlled Indexing</strong>
				<div>
					<!-- ngRepeat: term in ::keyword.kwd track by $index --><span ng-repeat="term in ::keyword.kwd track by $index" class="ng-binding ng-scope">
						<a href="/search/searchresult.jsp?matchBoolean=true&amp;queryText=&quot;Index%20Terms&quot;:.QT.size 0.13 mum.QT.&amp;newsearch=true" ng-bind-html="::term" data-tealium_data="{&quot;keywordType&quot;: &quot;INSPEC: Non-Controlled Indexing&quot;}" class="ng-binding">size 0.13 mum</a>, 
					</span><!-- end ngRepeat: term in ::keyword.kwd track by $index --><span ng-repeat="term in ::keyword.kwd track by $index" class="ng-binding ng-scope">
						<a href="/search/searchresult.jsp?matchBoolean=true&amp;queryText=&quot;Index%20Terms&quot;:.QT.inductorless low-noise amplifier.QT.&amp;newsearch=true" ng-bind-html="::term" data-tealium_data="{&quot;keywordType&quot;: &quot;INSPEC: Non-Controlled Indexing&quot;}" class="ng-binding">inductorless low-noise amplifier</a>,

<script>

function getCookie(name) {
	var nameEQ = name + "=";
	var ca = document.cookie.split(';');
	for(var i=0;i &lt; ca.length;i++) {
		var c = ca[i];
		while (c.charAt(0)==' ') c = c.substring(1,c.length);
		if (c.indexOf(nameEQ) == 0) return c.substring(nameEQ.length,c.length);
	}
	return null;
}

		if(IBP_WS_ENABLED_FLAG){
		Modal.refreshLegacyAccountTransition('/xpl/mwLegacyAccountTransition.jsp');
		}
	}
});

</script>

A current-reuse gain-enhanced noise canceling architecture is proposed, and the properties and limitations of the gain-enhancement stage are discussed. Capacitive peaking is employed to improve the gain flatness and -3-dB bandwidth, at the cost of absolute gain value. The LNA circuit is fabricated in a 0.13-mum triple-well CMOS technology. Measurement result shows that a small-signal gain of 11 dB and a -3-dB bandwidth of 2-9.6 GHz are obtained. Over the -3-dB bandwidth, the input return loss is less than -8.3 dB, and the noise figure is 3.6-4.8 dB. The LNA consumes 19 mW from a low supply voltage of 1.5 V. It is shown that the LNA designed without on-chip inductors achieves comparable performances with inductor-based designs. The silicon area is reduced significantly in the inductorless design, the LNA core occupies only 0.05 mm&lt;sup&gt;2&lt;/sup&gt;, which is among the smallest reported
